<div id="review_175053982" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/175053982" />
    <a title="Jason" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/783046-jason"><img alt="Jason" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548990073p2/783046.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/175053982?book_show_action=true">Jun 09, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason" href="/user/show/783046-jason">Jason</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/783046-jason?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/783046-jason?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/783046-jason?shelf=read-2011">read-2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/783046-jason?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer175053982" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9578733087691313271">About a year ago, I made a commitment to read all the H. P. Lovecraft I could find. Finding it all was easy. You can get the entire short stories and poetry, including some essays, all in one volume for your Amazon Kindle for something like ninety-nine cents. Reading it wasn't that difficult either. If you've never read Lovecraft, or even if you have and loved it, I would highly recommend that you read a short story then go over to The H. P. Lovecraft Literary Podcast and listen to the correspon</span>
  <span id="freeText9578733087691313271" style="display:none">About a year ago, I made a commitment to read all the H. P. Lovecraft I could find. Finding it all was easy. You can get the entire short stories and poetry, including some essays, all in one volume for your Amazon Kindle for something like ninety-nine cents. Reading it wasn't that difficult either. If you've never read Lovecraft, or even if you have and loved it, I would highly recommend that you read a short story then go over to The H. P. Lovecraft Literary Podcast and listen to the corresponding episode. Chris and Chad, who host the show, are both awesome. <br /><br />But I digress.<br /><br />H. P. Lovecraft is a fascinating writer of dark fantasy/science fiction/horror. Where I simply admired the man’s work earlier, he has now become a favorite. But I am now learning of even more fascinating authors; writers who are taking horrific and weird fiction in the vein of Lovecraft in entirely new and refreshing directions.<br /><br />One of these writers I’m speaking, of course, is Thomas Ligotti.<br /><br />As an avid reader, I've heard of Ligotti’s fiction for years now. His work has even been recommended to me many times, but for some reason I never got around to it. Such is life. I don’t know how many books I have in my to-be-read mountain, a lot of them from authors I've never read before. Thankfully, however, I recently decided to give Ligotti’s Teatro Grottesco a try. It, of course, is brilliant. I somehow knew that it would be, and so I regret not having read this man’s work before this year. <br /><br />What I like about Ligotti’s work the most is how much it seems to speak to me. Nearly every theme is a topic I have thought of more than once in one way or another. The overall landscape and pure beauty within the stories themselves is almost like opening my ribcage or cracking a hole in my skull and seeing what’s inside. Stories like Purity, The Town Manager, Sideshow and other stories, and The Red Tower were fascinating journeys into nightmares perhaps a little too familiar. Other stories, like My Case for Retributive Action and Our Temporary Supervisor come straight from my life.<br /><br />These stories are not simply stories. They are art. They are a mirror to any soul with substance. I look forward to reading more from Ligotti, and I can’t recommend him enough.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9578733087691313271" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer608996272" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4501700153410329996">Teatro Grottesco was the first short story collection I read by Ligotti. I must say that I am glad I read it, but happy to unmire myself from the unrelenting, bleak nightmare land of his visions.<br/>Was it a good read? Yes.<br/>Was it all I that it was hyped up to be? Not in my opinion.<br/>I did find many of the stories particularly delightful (The Red Tower, Gas Station Carnivals and Purity) but after several dips in the hopelesness of the collection, I found that it all became a bit repetitive.<br/>A couple of </span>
  <span id="freeText4501700153410329996" style="display:none">Teatro Grottesco was the first short story collection I read by Ligotti. I must say that I am glad I read it, but happy to unmire myself from the unrelenting, bleak nightmare land of his visions.<br />Was it a good read? Yes.<br />Was it all I that it was hyped up to be? Not in my opinion.<br />I did find many of the stories particularly delightful (The Red Tower, Gas Station Carnivals and Purity) but after several dips in the hopelesness of the collection, I found that it all became a bit repetitive.<br />A couple of friends have advised me that this isn't Ligotti's most triumphant offering, so I will no doubt revisit his work.<br />I gave this 3.5 and rounded up because the author's brilliant use of language, and because the darkness these stories live in felt so genuine. It was almost as if the author had tapped into my most depressing thoughts and painted their very somber origins on every page.</span>

<div id="review_2730744894" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2730744894" />
    <a title="S̶e̶a̶n̶" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/40012758-s-e-a-n"><img alt="S̶e̶a̶n̶" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1566235680p2/40012758.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2730744894?book_show_action=true">Mar 07, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="S̶e̶a̶n̶" class="user" itemprop="url" name="S̶e̶a̶n̶" href="/user/show/40012758-s-e-a-n">S̶e̶a̶n̶</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780753513743" href="/book/show/2472317.Teatro_Grottesco">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to S̶e̶a̶n̶ by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/35473855-jack-tripper">Jack Tripper</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40012758-s-e-a-n?shelf=2019">2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40012758-s-e-a-n?shelf=somewhere-else">somewhere-else</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40012758-s-e-a-n?shelf=weird-fiction">weird-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40012758-s-e-a-n?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2730744894" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2970046101141842002">Thomas Ligotti’s distinctive style maintains an intriguing continuity throughout much of this collection, with varying degrees of success in the choice of narrative vehicle, each of which runs on similar fuel: a stoic acceptance of the futility inherent in everything (excepting for a slight ambivalence toward the art that in turn acknowledges said futility). Having not read Ligotti before nor read about his influences, I was most curious to experience his style firsthand. Rather unexpectedly I d</span>
  <span id="freeText2970046101141842002" style="display:none">Thomas Ligotti’s distinctive style maintains an intriguing continuity throughout much of this collection, with varying degrees of success in the choice of narrative vehicle, each of which runs on similar fuel: a stoic acceptance of the futility inherent in everything (excepting for a slight ambivalence toward the art that in turn acknowledges said futility). Having not read Ligotti before nor read about his influences, I was most curious to experience his style firsthand. Rather unexpectedly I discovered more than a few similarities in his prose to that of the writer <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7745.Thomas_Bernhard" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Thomas Bernhard</a>, who did not write what one typically thinks of as <i>horror fiction</i>, but whose works certainly pulse with a vein of existential horror and share other themes explored by Ligotti, as well. The similarities noted between these two writers include the use of aloof, outsider narrators (who don’t differ much between stories, simply due to their common overbearing life-perspectives); construction of dense monologues composed of long, serpentine sentences; occasional reliance on secondhand narration; italicization of certain words and idiomatic expressions; repetition and reconstitution of phrases, often accompanied by the aggressive use of qualifiers; thematic concerns over pervasive anti-artistic or anti-intellectual sentiments; a recurring suicide fixation; and, more abstractly, the creation of a singular reading experience via total immersion in each narrator’s narrow, oppressive consciousness. <br /><br />To say I enjoyed these stories would be a mischaracterization of what happened as I read them. I do feel that I fully <i>experienced</i> these stories. Some of them affected me more than others. A few of the set-ups felt a bit too contrived, and I found some of the endings to fall on the weak side. Endings are one point where Ligotti and Bernhard differ in style. Bernhard rarely brings his novels to a definitive close; they often end quite suddenly. And I think Ligotti achieves the most success when he leaves his endings vague like this. Once such a monolithic narrative as these writers create is heaved onto a reader it is difficult for the writer to negotiate a way out, other than to abruptly shut it down. So, when Ligotti tries to add a twist it can be jarring. Not to say it always failed for me, because I really enjoyed some of the endings here, but when it did fail was when the execution felt too much like artifice. <br /><br />After reading most of the book and writing most of this review I finally succumbed to curiosity and read a few interviews with Ligotti. This actually aided my reading of the final long story, ‘The Shadow The Darkness’ (as well as others, in retrospect), for Ligotti is quite forthcoming in his interviews about his preoccupations and how they are manifested in his fiction. Perhaps more than any of the other stories in the collection, this final story illuminates my occasional disappointment with the direction Ligotti takes in his writing. Sometimes he reaches too far in an attempt to hit on all of his usual themes, which results in a muddied reading experience. ‘The Shadow The Darkness’ reads like satire of the egotistic machinations of artistic and literary circles by way of a perversion of Buddhist philosophy. It gets particularly murky toward the end, where there are simply too many ideological explorations and critiques converging at once to make for a satisfying conclusion. Instead we experience a simultaneous crying out against and resigned embrace of the resulting nullified existence. <br /><br />Though Ligotti has been described as a nihilist, he has countered that his chronic anhedonia precludes his ‘belief’ in nihilism or any other single philosophy or belief system. At the risk of tangling with intentional fallacy (which in this case I would argue is largely irrelevant, based on Ligotti’s interviews), it is the experience of existing in a state completely devoid of pleasure that so deeply pervades many of the stories in this book.<blockquote><i>But I wanted to believe that the artist who created these dream monologues about the bungalow house and the derelict factory had not set out to break my heart or anyone’s heart. I wanted to believe that this artist had escaped the dreams and demons of all</i> sentiment<i> in order to explore the foul and crummy delights of a universe where everything had been reduced to three stark principles: first, that there was nowhere for you to go; second, that there was nothing for you to do; and third, that there was no one for you to know. Of course I knew that this view was an illusion like any other, but it was also one that had sustained me so long and so well – as long and as well as any other illusion and perhaps longer, perhaps better.</i></blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="2970046101141842002" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_164224998" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/164224998" />
    <a title="Nate D" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/406701-nate-d"><img alt="Nate D" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1203897989p2/406701.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/164224998?book_show_action=true">Apr 28, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nate D" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nate D" href="/user/show/406701-nate-d">Nate D</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780753513743" href="/book/show/2472317.Teatro_Grottesco">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">artists who burn their work and disappear</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Nate D by: </span>
      carnies and fortune-tellers
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=read-in-2011">read-in-2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=stories">stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=horror">horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer164224998" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5604601011994505735">It's entirely unsurprising to learn that Thomas Ligotti is from Detroit. His storytelling is suffused in a certain distinctly post-industrial sense of destruction and despair. This context is especially prevalent in the neighborhood descriptions of opening tour-de-force "Purity" which shoves several disquieting philosophical principles through a slalom of screwed-up events, ranging from explicit action to entirely sub-narrative suggestions. All told in the conversational voice of an eerily unfaz</span>
  <span id="freeText5604601011994505735" style="display:none">It's entirely unsurprising to learn that Thomas Ligotti is from Detroit. His storytelling is suffused in a certain distinctly post-industrial sense of destruction and despair. This context is especially prevalent in the neighborhood descriptions of opening tour-de-force "Purity" which shoves several disquieting philosophical principles through a slalom of screwed-up events, ranging from explicit action to entirely sub-narrative suggestions. All told in the conversational voice of an eerily unfazable child narrator, too used to his family to register what goes on around him, perhaps. It's a fantastic story all around. It's also the least typical. The best aspect of the others is the endless narratorial ruminations which explore Ligotti's unsettling concepts, an excellent literary device aptly likened to Thomas Bernhard. The worst aspect of the other stories is that they seem to largely depend on the sideshow theme encapsulated by the collection title. Why Ligotti feels the need to delve into such horror genre cliches as creepy puppets and carnivals and showmanship seems entirely inexplicable to me, but it's not just a recurring element. It actually seems to be the chosen unifying concept to the collection, like his later "tales of corporate horror". That Ligotti's conceptual goals for these overused devices are so weird and removed from their usual appearances only partly helps. I can think of ways of justifying them, none of which are actually preferable to <i>just not using cliches at all</i>. It's just unneccessary and allows a suggestion of camp into his work that is entirely at odds with the actual content. Of course, Ligotti is often an amazing writer of prodigious imagination, so it's pretty easy to overlook these issues in the enjoyment of individual stories like "The Town Manager", "Severini", and "Gas Station Carnivals". I just can't completely get a read on his goals here.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5604601011994505735" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_32552627" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/32552627" />
    <a title="Tim Pendry" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1016626-tim-pendry"><img alt="Tim Pendry" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1210701989p2/1016626.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/32552627?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tim Pendry" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tim Pendry" href="/user/show/1016626-tim-pendry">Tim Pendry</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone Strong</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1016626-tim-pendry?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1016626-tim-pendry?shelf=five-star">five-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1016626-tim-pendry?shelf=north-american">north-american</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer32552627" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4646296402488455161">Thomas Ligotti's work has been hard to find in the UK. When I picked up this edition (published by Virgin in 2008 from the 2006 US hardback), I feared that it would be another general anthology largely duplicating the only other available text - The Shadow At The Bottom Of The World. <br/><br/>Of course, there are very many overlaps (most notably Purity, The Red Tower, The Bungalow House, Severini and Teatro Grottesco itself) but the two books are complementary and not competitive. Why? The 'Shadow' (to b</span>
  <span id="freeText4646296402488455161" style="display:none">Thomas Ligotti's work has been hard to find in the UK. When I picked up this edition (published by Virgin in 2008 from the 2006 US hardback), I feared that it would be another general anthology largely duplicating the only other available text - The Shadow At The Bottom Of The World. <br /><br />Of course, there are very many overlaps (most notably Purity, The Red Tower, The Bungalow House, Severini and Teatro Grottesco itself) but the two books are complementary and not competitive. Why? The 'Shadow' (to be reviewed separately) presents a series of small masterpieces that give a taste of this heir to Poe in the round but 'Teatro' presents Ligotti as a coherent philosophy of life - or is it non-life since the mood is decidely nihilistic.<br /><br />Three sections presents different viewpoints on an essentially single dark vision of existence - a form of anti-existentialist nihilism in which the life force at the heart of the universe is presented as something very dark indeed, a deadening puppetry in which blind forces make us act or become as they wish and not as we, illusorily, believe we will ourselves to do or to be.<br /><br />All this is encapsulated in the final story - 'The Shadow, The Darkness' - which requires what has gone before in the book and leaves one with a taste of a nihilism that must surely cause us to worry about Mr. Ligotti's mental state. I have never read a more credible account of that blackest state of existence, existential depression - one without Satanic or demonic dark forces, a pure expression of (perhaps) Lovecraft's Nyarlothep's malign and meaningless rule over all being.<br /><br />This alone is not a story to be read lightly - certainly not by someone mentally vulnerable. Personally, I found it powerful, a necessary read and one that triggered a sense of commitment to life in me precisely because, in literary form, it exhibited the darkest mental state imaginable, Styron's and Milton's 'darkness visible'. To see this darkness, feel it inside oneself as a possibility and then turn away from it is a liberation - but some may not be able to do that. Approach with care if you are unsure of your own hold on life.<br /><br />But we run ahead of ourselves ... The book is divided into three sections - Derangements, Deformations, and The Damaged and the Diseased. <br /><br />The first [Derangements] comprises five stories of alienation and, since I am not into spoilers, let us just say that he presents a world of individuals and communities where the protagonists are victims or observers of various mysterious, unfathomable, apparently random, external forces whose general tendency is towards madness, dissolution, degradation and death. <br /><br />This is Lovecraft shorn of even the hope that there might be a heroic conclusion or an ultimate triumph of the ordinary against dark forces. The Old Ones are no longer 'personalities' out there to be feared and fought but have become diffuse mindless malignities inside us and our communities. Dark forces are embedded very deep indeed within the ordinary and seem to be loki-like in their conduct, engaged in a snide and sardonic sideshow at our expense. Malicious almost out of boredom, accident and blind nature. Indeed, the hint is that nature, our nature, is 'nihil' or, at best, a blind willing of itself at our expense.<br /><br />The second [Deformations] set of three stories are centred on a very particular environment - a mysterious and foggy border town where a faceless corporation is in league with manipulative forces, the medical profession it would seem, to pull in unsuspecting and mentally vulnerable people and force them into lives of inescapable drudgery 'lightened' only by random acts of cruelty which seem to be without personal malice. An air of cosmic malice depersonalises the horror.<br /><br />Existence is seen as almost accidental, a situation where all we can see are the workings of systems that cannot be understood, operating according to unknown rules and rivalries, that entrap us as readers to the degree that we identify with the neurasthenic, paranoid personalities who act as narrators. Hidden within the text are hints of the sort of practices that Lovecraft and Howard would have assigned to 'unspeakable cults'.<br /><br />If the first section is about the horror of being at the mercy of specific events and processes and the second is seemingly an allegory of the meaningless of modern life, working as drudges towards death, the real darkness, centred on art and philosophy, comes in the third section, 'The Damaged and the Diseased'. This culminates in the vile, dark tale with which this review began.<br /><br />Again no spoilers but the five stories seem to centre on the presumption of art and of the intellect in their claims to come close to meaning in a meaningless existence. The characters are possibly all mad, certainly disturbed and often sick in body as much as mind, introvert and stuck in their dreams or in environments that are claustrophobic. The best known work is Teatro Grottesco, a descriptor of the world of Ligotti, as much as the story 'The Clown Puppet' is a descriptor of the individual in that world. There are clowns, carnivals, costumes, cabarets, theatres, puppetry, art, all supposed to be providers of fun and entertainment but all, following the tradition of Stephen King and Ray Bradbury, twisted into paranoia, deception, madness and tragedy.<br /><br />Why go on? The world of Ligotti is disturbing. I have mentioned the obvious influence of Poe mediated by Lovecraft. There are small nods to King (an underestimated writer) although Ligotti writes in small amounts to concentrated effect whereas King tells long rollicking tales that somehow always seem to affirm humanity somewhere in the text, even if the general tone is pessimistic. <br /><br />In my view, Ligotti is a must-read and a re-read and the fact that the book can contain perhaps half the material of another anthology and yet the two anthologies bear complete separate (possibly repeated) readings (like Poe and Lovecraft) suggests that the artistry lies in the positioning of the stories against one another as much as in the stories themselves. But for a taste, I give you with two extended quotations:-<br /><br />"Even in a northern border town of such intensely chaotic oddity and corruption there was still some greater chaos, some deeper insanity, than one had counted on, or could ever be taken into account - wherever there was anything, there would be chaos and insanity to such a degree that one could never come to terms with it, and it was only a matter of time before your world, whatever you thought it to be, was undermined, if not completely overrun, by another world."<br />['In A Foreign Town, In A Foreign Land']<br /><br />"Words are a total obfuscation of the most basic fact of existence, the very conspiracy against the human race ... Words are simply a cover-up for this conspiracy. They are the ultimate means for the cover-up, the ultimate artwork of the shadow, the darkness - its ultimate artistic cover-up. Because of the existence of words, we think that there exists a mind, that some kind of self or soul exists. This is just another of the infinite layers of the cover-up'<br /><br />['The Shadow, The Darkness']<br /><br />There is one last thing to note - the style. Ligotti uses repetitions a lot, much like Lovecraft might use a word like 'eldritch' to make the reader feel some intensity of engagement with the story. The style is hard to pin down but it is detached and world-weary, observing, emotionless, sometimes allusive so that some horror or disturbance is seen (as it were) slightly offstage, like a twitch of a curtain or rat running across the aisle of the show. The mood is of being stuck in a grey, misty or sepia stage set or geographical location where, if you walked too far in any direction, you might fall off the ends of the known world and into nothingness. Those borders seem to shrink as the stories progress so that claustrophobia is inevitable.<br /><br />There is something European about the sensibility. Ligotti is not in the least optimistic like your average American. This is the world of Dr. Caligari and black and white art movies of long ago, of Kafka (one is reminded of 'In the Penal Colony') ... there are a lot of hospitals and medication, a lot of attempts to avoid reality through art, work, society and drugs that are ultimately useless.<br /><br />So, if you have a strong stomach for existential horror, try it.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4646296402488455161" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_317539735" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/317539735" />
    <a title="Jean-marcel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8821840-jean-marcel"><img alt="Jean-marcel" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/317539735?book_show_action=true">Apr 23, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jean-marcel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jean-marcel" href="/user/show/8821840-jean-marcel">Jean-marcel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer317539735" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5661179200560847454"><b>On Reading Thomas Ligotti: A Confession</b><br/><br/>over a space of many months, I poured over .... no, agonized over ... the words of this cold, aloof misanthrope. It did not lighten my feelings about the world in the slightest. But in a strange, difficult to elucidate manner, it made me feel better about myself.<br/><br/>There have been points in life when your humble reviewer has been called "negative", "pessimistic", "dark", "emotionally stunted", "lacking in affection". Sometime in my late teen years, I even star</span>
  <span id="freeText5661179200560847454" style="display:none"><b>On Reading Thomas Ligotti: A Confession</b><br /><br />over a space of many months, I poured over .... no, agonized over ... the words of this cold, aloof misanthrope. It did not lighten my feelings about the world in the slightest. But in a strange, difficult to elucidate manner, it made me feel better about myself.<br /><br />There have been points in life when your humble reviewer has been called "negative", "pessimistic", "dark", "emotionally stunted", "lacking in affection". Sometime in my late teen years, I even started believing all these things about myself. I've had girlfriends break up with me because, they said, I was "too dark" for them. As I've gotten older, I've realized a few things. Expressions of love and humanity do make me smile. I enjoy what you might call 'dark art", but at my core, I'm really nothing but a softy who loves kittens, thinks the achievements of humanity are as likely to be beautiful as terrible, and who has come to realize that going around thinking the worst of everyone all the time is an utterly fucking exhausting way to live. I may be reserved in my enthusiasms, but I enjoy being happy, and those girls really just didn't take the trouble to get to know what I was really like, or else perhaps I was just a bit too reserved to show it.<br /><br /> What about Thomas Ligotti?<br /><br />Well, he obviously has plenty of literary antecedents. Poe and Lovecraft are ones he himself has mentioned, and I can definitely feel the resonance of both in his work. The glimpses of stark and unutterably inimical cosmic horror remind me of Lovecraft, whereas the dry and sometimes pedantic (mostly in a cool way though) manner of writing makes me think of Poe. He has an obsession with puppets. Sometimes it's a literal manifestation in his work, and sometimes it's more metaphorical, as in the "corporate horror" tales. I think Ligotti believes that human beings are all pathetic puppets, actually, except he doesn't really have time or inclination for pathos. In fact, a visceral disgust and contempt seems to drift from the pages of his work like some kind of chilly miasmic cloud. <br /><br />When I read lovecraft, I feel like I'm on the outside looking in. I enjoy myself despite the horror, you might say. When I read Stephen King, I feel like he's really a sentimental sop at heart despite whatever madness he might choose to write about. When I read Poe, sometimes I feel like I'm being educated about the human mind, and people always look at me funny when I try to explain to them that there's a hell of a lot of humour in plenty of Poe's work. When I take a deep breath and dive into a Thomas Ligotti story, I feel like he hates everything about this existence and that maybe the most satisfying thing would be to cut everyone's puppet strings by creating a nuclear holocaust, except by doing that you'd probably still be acting out the nebulous and insane will of a Blind Idiot God. Futility. Degradation of the spirit. Reaching for the transcendent and finding a sewer instead. Horror of a kind that worms into your soul and leaves you feeling a bit tenuous and shaky.<br /><br />The writing is oddly subtle while simultaneously a bit browbeating about its bleakness. Ligotti repeats phrases like mantras within various stories, which leads to some sentences being a bit tortuous and long. Sometimes I get the feeling while reading that I'm turning into some sort of desperate, scrabbling animal. The grotesqueries in the stories are sometimes visceral, but more often they're of an all-too-human sort. What is so horrible about "The Town manager"? I just can't explain it and the only thing is to read it and come up with your own conclusions. Just what is going on in "Purity"? I am not quite sure, but there are hints and allusions that made me shiver, and the fact that this story came the closest to having "real" characters in it (a very strange and dysfunctional family) made it seem somehow more immediate and less aloof than much of Ligotti's writing. Why does "The Red Tower" keep churning out horrible, morbid toys? Because humanity asked for it, I guess.<br /><br />"Our Temporary Supervisor" made me feel depressed about going to work for weeks. I may not be spending time screwing together "little pieces of metal" for unknown purposes but sometimes moving bits and bytes along at a desk must feel even less productive, surely? Do I have to medicate myself just to keep going? How long before I'm too afraid, and the "greater things in life" I thought I was destined for before I started working at The Factory...er...The Office...become more and more shadowy, until not even my dreams can touch them? Shades of <i>The Prisoner</i> here, or maybe Robert Aickman at his most obtuse. Very, very disquieting.<br /><br />So what I was trying to say in my opening: a rare glimpse into myself, is that Ligotti taught me something. he taught me that I'm not really anywhere close to being a nihilist. I have a fascination with reading the stories and indeed will probably read many more, and yet on some primal level I can't help but shy away from them. I almost feel like that's a good thing. Perhaps I am falling into the Puppet master's trap. Thanks, TL, though; it's honestly a bit relieving.<br /><br />On reflection: Bumped the rating up to four stars, because I like to base the ratings on how a work impacted me in particular, and I think these stories hit me harder and will stay with me longer than a three-star score would suggest.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5661179200560847454" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_41435934" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/41435934" />
    <a title="Ben Loory" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/171197-ben-loory"><img alt="Ben Loory" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1492548411p2/171197.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/41435934?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ben Loory" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ben Loory" href="/user/show/171197-ben-loory">Ben Loory</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780753513743" href="/book/show/2472317.Teatro_Grottesco">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer41435934" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17534966107838229023"><br/>sometimes i come upon books or stories that make me question the nature of reality; that make me wonder about the possibility of other realms of existence, other ways of seeing or living or being, etc. but reading ligotti, i often get the sense that not only do these things absolutely exist, but that ligotti is speaking to me-- directly to me personally-- from that other realm, through the medium of this book, this story, as though taunting me to step forward and enter that place with him. it is</span>
  <span id="freeText17534966107838229023" style="display:none"><br />sometimes i come upon books or stories that make me question the nature of reality; that make me wonder about the possibility of other realms of existence, other ways of seeing or living or being, etc. but reading ligotti, i often get the sense that not only do these things absolutely exist, but that ligotti is speaking to me-- directly to me personally-- from that other realm, through the medium of this book, this story, as though taunting me to step forward and enter that place with him. it is somewhat terrifying. (luckily at those moments there is myspace.)<br /><br />anyway. the blurb on the front of this, his most recent collection of stories, marks him as being in the tradition of h.p. lovecraft, which is stupid and untrue. lovecraft is above all a believer in science and reality, even as they go up against the dark forces of the irrational and the unknown. ligotti belongs to the family that includes poe, kafka, and aickman... his stories exist in nightmare realms where the only question is: what level of hell are we currently on?<br /><br />in a way, i feel sorry for ligotti, in that he gets shelved in the horror section, where he remains unread and unrecognized partly because he writes only short stories and partly because of his formidable intellect and refusal to commercialize his work. there are no slashers in ligotti stories, no recognizable monsters, no heroes, no innocent little children to save. often it is hard to tell what, if anything, has happened; what the meaning is. and yet the stories themselves are incredibly clear and vivid. there are repeated motifs: failed artists who haven’t yet realized the extent of their failures; strange, empty hospitals and coffeeshops; back-alleys and libraries and old boarding houses; persons suffering from gastro-intestinal upset (i imagine ligotti forever clutching a bottle of pepto-bismol); unpleasant and inexplicable workplace conditions; the absolute and total absence of even the mention of the word love; oh yeah, and marionettes and puppets and stage shows of all shapes and sizes (freak shows, magic shows, parades, performance art pieces). there’s nothing here: no hope, nothing to do, no reason to be alive (if, indeed, any of "us" are alive). just a kind of empty circus that no one laughs at, even though it is amazingly imaginative and perfect and horribly beautiful in every way.<br /><br />i want to say that ligotti doesn’t so much stare into the void, as as the void, but if he actually was the void, he probably wouldn’t be writing. let’s just say he seems to largely be comprised of the void. it gets a little oppressive after a while, but the imagery and ideas and language are so great, it’s hard to read just one...</span>
  <a data-text-id="17534966107838229023" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1058572969" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1058572969" />
    <a title="Paul  Perry" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4107783-paul-perry"><img alt="Paul  Perry" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1606145814p2/4107783.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1058572969?book_show_action=true">Sep 18, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul  Perry" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul  Perry" href="/user/show/4107783-paul-perry">Paul  Perry</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/12258969-teatro-grottesco">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4107783-paul-perry?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4107783-paul-perry?shelf=male-author">male-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4107783-paul-perry?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4107783-paul-perry?shelf=american">american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4107783-paul-perry?shelf=read-in-2014">read-in-2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1058572969" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16392492047477593965">This is a difficult book to rate. As my earlier updates suggest, I started of very much liking it (although 'liking' may not be the right word, given the thoroughly unsettling nature of the stories). The opening story was utterly superb, and the quality continued through the next couple of sections - the book is broken into short collections of themed stories, the tales in each related to a greater or lesser extent - but, toward the end, I was beginning to find a sameness to the writing rather w</span>
  <span id="freeText16392492047477593965" style="display:none">This is a difficult book to rate. As my earlier updates suggest, I started of very much liking it (although 'liking' may not be the right word, given the thoroughly unsettling nature of the stories). The opening story was utterly superb, and the quality continued through the next couple of sections - the book is broken into short collections of themed stories, the tales in each related to a greater or lesser extent - but, toward the end, I was beginning to find a sameness to the writing rather wearing.<br /><br />The early sections are superb. Derangements is thoroughly surreal and creepy, with The Town Manager in particular having a circular element that reminded me an especially good, odd Twilight Zone. The tales in Defamations deal with a corporate horror, a weirdly Kafka-esque world where yu are utterly controlled by your employment which seeks only productivity and drains all other motivation from life. It made me think of Robert Chambers but in a pointedly modern, industrial setting.<br /><br />It was the Teatro Grottesco stories themselves, forming the final section, that for me spoiled the collection. A sense that had been building from some of the previous stories that the narrative voices - always first person - were too similar too each other, all of a somewhat pompous, overly-wordy circumlocution that made me think of 19th century literature, did become a problem. ~The stories themselves I found less interesting, the ordinary people of the earlier stories replaced by bohemian artistic types further making the stories feel more dated and less relevant. The stories seemed rather pointless - in the way that some weaker Lovecraftian horror is, where rather than the feeling of hopelessness there is a sense of "huh?" - although the denouement of the final story did reverse this with a growing crescendo of dread and a final burst - or perhaps seep would be a better word - of existential terror.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16392492047477593965" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_510042332" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/510042332" />
    <a title="Randolph" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1804559-randolph"><img alt="Randolph" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1544247008p2/1804559.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/510042332?book_show_action=true">Jan 18, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Randolph" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Randolph" href="/user/show/1804559-randolph">Randolph</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1804559-randolph?shelf=strange-stories">strange-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1804559-randolph?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1804559-randolph?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer510042332" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10110264399088889847">Kafka on steroids. I didn't like this book as much as I thought I would because, although I love Kafka, I've moved on in how I think fiction should address the nihilistic worldview. I'm in the Harlan Ellison camp where the best stories have flesh and blood characters that we actually care about. The stories were weird and somewhat disturbing but never creepy or scary. The atmosphere is more absurdist than horror. Ligotti is definitely unique in his fictional translation of the ultimate meaningle</span>
  <span id="freeText10110264399088889847" style="display:none">Kafka on steroids. I didn't like this book as much as I thought I would because, although I love Kafka, I've moved on in how I think fiction should address the nihilistic worldview. I'm in the Harlan Ellison camp where the best stories have flesh and blood characters that we actually care about. The stories were weird and somewhat disturbing but never creepy or scary. The atmosphere is more absurdist than horror. Ligotti is definitely unique in his fictional translation of the ultimate meaningless of life. I suppose some people would find this "horrifying" and I understand and appreciate what he is trying to do but it's just not part of my belief system so it doesn't work as well for me as say Lovecraft, or better yet, Ellison does. I'm also more frightened of the bogeyman in my closet than I am of the vapory supervisor in the corner office. If he spent more time developing his characters and making me care for them then I would probably find their meaningless lives more tragic.<br /><br />I see Kafka here not Lovecraft. It starts at the same place: an uncaring neutral cosmos, but is developed in a more everyman-type Kafka way, not into horror or science fiction.<br /><br />Some of the stories are actually quite funny, at least to me.<br /><br />Worth reading if just for Ligotti's unique vision.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10110264399088889847" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_35071778" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/35071778" />
    <a title="Sam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1340558-sam"><img alt="Sam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1341494491p2/1340558.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/35071778?book_show_action=true">Oct 11, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sam" href="/user/show/1340558-sam">Sam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer35071778" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8285502543391569012">I am a relative newcomer to Thomas Ligotti, but I love his work. Evocative and cerebral, his stories conjure feelings of dread and surreal alienation. I think this is why H. P. Lovecraft is so often mentioned in connection with Ligotti; their styles are vastly different, but work the same empty and dimly-lit back alleys of our emotional cores.<br/><br/>I believe this is the only Ligotti book currently in print, and it is only the second I have read (after "The Shadow at the Bottom of the World"). Like a l</span>
  <span id="freeText8285502543391569012" style="display:none">I am a relative newcomer to Thomas Ligotti, but I love his work. Evocative and cerebral, his stories conjure feelings of dread and surreal alienation. I think this is why H. P. Lovecraft is so often mentioned in connection with Ligotti; their styles are vastly different, but work the same empty and dimly-lit back alleys of our emotional cores.<br /><br />I believe this is the only Ligotti book currently in print, and it is only the second I have read (after "The Shadow at the Bottom of the World"). Like a lot of short story collections, some stories appear in more than one book, and there are indeed a few in here that were included in "...Shadow...", but "Teatro Grottesco" is still well worth the cover price.<br /><br />A few of the stories take place in a shared setting, near the foreboding border of a country controlled by an omnipresent company. Far from cyberpunk sci-fi, these stories have a rich old-fashioned tinge, and are some of my favorites.<br /><br />If you tend to be attracted to things dark and offbeat, you owe it to yourself to check out Thomas Ligotti.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8285502543391569012" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1342964247" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1342964247" />
    <a title="Sakib" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38866986-sakib"><img alt="Sakib" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564417072p2/38866986.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1342964247?book_show_action=true">Feb 18, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sakib" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sakib" href="/user/show/38866986-sakib">Sakib</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/12258969-teatro-grottesco">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38866986-sakib?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38866986-sakib?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38866986-sakib?shelf=horror-weird-fiction">horror-weird-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38866986-sakib?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1342964247" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10885659928144482763"><b>"And no matter what I say cannot resist or betray it. No one could do so because there is no one here. There is only this body, this shadow, this darkness."</b><br/><br/>I remember picking this one up several years ago, and reading the first story <b>Purity</b>, and putting it down for reasons I can't really describe without feeling a bit ashamed...<br/><br/>Obviously, I did't get it; I wasn't ready, and I had better things to do like picking up "better" books... and by "better" I mean the ones that could be interesting to me</span>
  <span id="freeText10885659928144482763" style="display:none"><b>"And no matter what I say cannot resist or betray it. No one could do so because there is no one here. There is only this body, this shadow, this darkness."</b><br /><br />I remember picking this one up several years ago, and reading the first story <b>Purity</b>, and putting it down for reasons I can't really describe without feeling a bit ashamed...<br /><br />Obviously, I did't get it; I wasn't ready, and I had better things to do like picking up "better" books... and by "better" I mean the ones that could be interesting to me...<br /><br />But it's also a fact that it somewhat disturbed me; although I didn't fully understand everything, or the story as a "whole", my intuitions made me leave the book and move on.<br /><br />This story was my introduction to weird fiction.<br /><br />Now years later I'm on a train of weird thoughts, with weird impulses and desires that are making me read these disfigured tales of the very definitions of figures we see in our normal lives...<br /><br />I had higher ambitions and with it the fear that followed before reading this book, after finishing <b>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10350530.The_Man_Who_Collected_Machen_and_Other_Weird_Tales" title="The Man Who Collected Machen and Other Weird Tales by Mark Samuels" rel="nofollow noopener">The Man Who Collected Machen and Other Weird Tales</a>
</b>.<br /><br />I have to say I truly enjoyed the book...<br /><br />Unlike Mark Samuels, the author of the previous book that I read and of course the one and only Lovecraft, Thomas Ligotti, as evident from numerous articles about his literature and style, doesn't present you with an "implicit" kind of horror that you might see coming at you: with all the apparent violence, bodily abominations, strange figures and whatnot. It's more or less just like as S.T. Joshi said: <b>"philosophical horror"</b><br /><br />Ligotti's storytelling is such a crafty one, with a structure that contains repetitions making his work a soothing parade of metafiction, his prose like a good-nurtured diary of records depicting random and unique kind of horrors that no one's faced, thought or read before. His prose sinks in deep, deeper and more...<br /><br />And sometimes the storytelling gets so cruelly humorous- with remarkable apathy and brilliant word choice...<br /><br />I won't be able to give a detailed review of every story, since they are, at least in my case, not like any other things that I've read; every story, in a way, has worked inside me with both comprehension and incomprehension, with all the contents inside (and outside) them. <br /><br />I like how the book's arranged. So the first five stories in the <b>Derangements</b> section:<br /><br />I liked <b>Purity</b>, although I'm not sure I get it fully still now, or what the heck happened at the end, but it's just as disturbing...<br /><br />The <b>The Town Manager</b> is a very symbolic and thoughtful work in the field of horror and weird fiction, as I understand and read in a review of it. The next one, <b>Sideshow, and Other Stories</b>, is just as hazy to me. There are little stories inside that are worth pondering over. <b>The Clown Puppet</b> is another work that that mostly has been out of my depth whenever I try to decipher its inner meaning. <b>The Red Tower</b> will most likely make you want to look at old buildings in a different perspective. <br /><br />And my most favorite stories are here, in the next part called <b>Deformations</b>...<br />The first two stories and inter-related, inter-connected and conjures an amazing tale. The next story, <b>In a Strange Town, In a Strange Land</b>, is a delirious journey, depicting a strange place and strange occurrences, from where you can never come back wholly...<br /><br />The last section, <b>The Damaged and The Deceased</b>, contains five stories that are all somehow connected with art, with weirder but more easy-to-grasp contents, with philosophical overtones getting denser in each story, until culminating in the last one.<br /><br />I intend to go for the author's next books, preferably his more notable and acclaimed collections published in the nineties and eighties, as quickly as I can. <br /><br />I'm going to stop my nonsense review with a quote that would suit much:<br /><br /><b>"It has always seemed to me that my existence consisted purely and exclusively of nothing but the most outrageous nonsense."</b><br /><br /><b>
  <i>Upon second time reading the book, I must give it a five-star rating; It has impacted and moved me so much profoundly and deeply... I haven't felt this much compelled to revisit a work as I have in this case, and rarely such a satisfying one.</i>
</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="10885659928144482763" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_299758238" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/299758238" />
    <a title="Ed [Redacted]" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3968676-ed-redacted"><img alt="Ed [Redacted]" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1429513592p2/3968676.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/299758238?book_show_action=true">Mar 24, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ed [Redacted]" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ed [Redacted]" href="/user/show/3968676-ed-redacted">Ed [Redacted]</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3968676-ed-redacted?shelf=2012">2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer299758238" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17960704733619561598">Great selection of seriously bleak stories about how you can't do a god damned thing to save yourself from a bitter end at the hands of the inexorable forces of the cosmos. Not exactly uplifting I guess is what I'm trying to say. The stories are often haunting and I still thought about many of these stories hours and/or days after reading them. "The Clown Puppet" and “In Foreign Town, in a Foreign Country.” are two standouts in a collection with no truly bad stories. Ligotti is kind of cross bet</span>
  <span id="freeText17960704733619561598" style="display:none">Great selection of seriously bleak stories about how you can't do a god damned thing to save yourself from a bitter end at the hands of the inexorable forces of the cosmos. Not exactly uplifting I guess is what I'm trying to say. The stories are often haunting and I still thought about many of these stories hours and/or days after reading them. "The Clown Puppet" and “In Foreign Town, in a Foreign Country.” are two standouts in a collection with no truly bad stories. Ligotti is kind of cross between Lovecraft, Kafka and Sartre (all Absurd and no Authenticity though). This kind of stuff is right up my alley though it may not be for everyone. Loved it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17960704733619561598" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2632258459" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2632258459" />
    <a title="Andrew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/442654-andrew"><img alt="Andrew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1513093267p2/442654.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2632258459?book_show_action=true">Jan 11, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew" href="/user/show/442654-andrew">Andrew</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/442654-andrew?shelf=american-short-stories">american-short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/442654-andrew?shelf=2000s-american-fiction">2000s-american-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/442654-andrew?shelf=american-fiction">american-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/442654-andrew?shelf=american-genre-fiction">american-genre-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2632258459" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17723014245408604062">A marked improvement over Ligotti's earlier stories -- as a mature writer, he forsakes many of the silly Lovecraftian twitches in favor of a strong, dread-laden atmosphere. While this might show up in the horror section, it is far, far more universal, and it speaks to the kind of horror one experiences when diving into open water on the ocean rather than the horror of vampires and bogeymen. I can't recommend this highly enough.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('13uqSWkSy+Qdt4URUK3sxSfz0Jtt8BYHnWSsp0jKhvnaNCT+HENVVlaLGYShD/BXb1KtkZY4i+1SVu2nYbhc2w==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

In this book, Eugene Thacker suggests that we look to the genre of horror as offering a way of thinking about the unthinkable world. To confront this idea is to confront the limit of our ability to understand the world in which we live—a central motif of the horror genre.\n\nIn the Dust of This Planet explores these relationships between philosophy and horror. In Thacker\'s hands, philosophy is not academic logic-chopping; instead, it is the thought of the limit of all thought, especially as it dovetails into occultism, demonology, and mysticism. Likewise, Thacker takes horror to mean something beyond the focus on gore and scare tactics, but as the under-appreciated genre of supernatural horror in fiction, film, comics, and music.<\/span>\n Perhaps no figure better embodies the transition from the Gothic tradition to modern horror than Arthur Machen. In the final decade of the nineteenth century, the Welsh writer produced a seminal body of tales of occult horror, spiritual and physical corruption, and malignant survivals from the primeval past which horrified and scandalized late-Victorian readers. Machen\'s &quot;weird fiction&quot; has influenced generations of storytellers, from H. P. Lovecraft to Guillermo Del Toro - and it remains no less unsettling today. This new collection, which includes the complete novel The Three Impostors as well as such celebrated tales as The Great God Pan and The White People, constitutes the most comprehensive critical edition of Machen yet to appear. In addition to the core late-Victorian horror classics, a selection of lesser-known prose poems and later tales helps to present a fuller picture of the development of Machen\'s weird vision. All are faced with the loneliest corners of themselves and strive to find an escape.Nathan Ballingrud was born in Massachusetts but has spent most of his life in the South. Throughout the stories the reader is introduced to a variety of characters, from a man who spends the night in a Hospice to a German aristocrat and a woman who sees an image of her own soul. In these 11 stories, the occasion may be a walking tour of Northern England, a birthday present of a Victorian dollhouse or a stay at a Swedish sanatorium for insomniacs, but it simultaneously traps the characters with dread and opens them up to a new awareness of a greater, deeper and more dangerous world. The story follows Kline, a brutally dismembered detective forcibly recruited to solve a murder inside the cul<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText1223523187021542230\" style=\"display:none\">Intense and profoundly unsettling, Brian Evenson’s Last Days is a down-the-rabbit-hole detective novel set in an underground religious cult. The story follows Kline, a brutally dismembered detective forcibly recruited to solve a murder inside the cult. As Kline becomes more deeply involved with the group, he begins to realize the stakes are higher than he previously thought. Attempting to find his way through a maze of lies, threats, and misinformation, Kline discovers that his survival depends on an act of sheer will.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/128466.Thomas_Ligotti">About Thomas Ligotti</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/128466.Thomas_Ligotti"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1371462738p3/128466.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/128466.Thomas_Ligotti">Thomas Ligotti
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
1,853
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer16009711076847059611"><b>Thomas Ligotti</b> is a contemporary American horror author and reclusive literary cult figure. His writings, while unique in style, have been noted as major continuations of several literary genres—most prominently Lovecraftian horror—and have overall been described as works of "philosophical horror", often written as philosophical novels with a "darker" undertone which is similar to gothic fiction. </span>
  <span id="freeText16009711076847059611" style="display:none"><b>Thomas Ligotti</b> is a contemporary American horror author and reclusive literary cult figure. His writings, while unique in style, have been noted as major continuations of several literary genres—most prominently Lovecraftian horror—and have overall been described as works of "philosophical horror", often written as philosophical novels with a "darker" undertone which is similar to gothic fiction. <i>The Washington Post</i> called him "the best kept secret in contemporary horror fiction"; another critic declared "It's a skilled writer indeed who can suggest a horror so shocking that one is grateful it was kept offstage."</span>
  <a data-text-id="16009711076847059611" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>
